The storage layout, the deposit/withdraw interface, and the LP accounting all stay exactly the same. The upgrade is additive, not structural. Deploy JS so is it okay to replace the new panatly 8ball and turnament contracts with the new once you gave me 5:19 AM Evaluated deployment status and confirmed new contracts safe to deploy Evaluated deployment status and confirmed new contracts safe to deploy Yes, and here's the exact status of each one: EightBallVerifier_Complete and PenaltyVerifier_Complete — these are the same files you uploaded. I haven't touched them. They're being deployed fresh for the first time, no replacement involved. Tournament_REALM — same file you uploaded, unchanged. Also a fresh deploy filling an empty slot in CrossRealmNFT. TournamentBracket → TournamentBracketV2 — this is the only real swap. Your original TournamentBracket.sol was never deployed on-chain, so there is nothing to migrate, no state to preserve, no users to impact. You're deploying V2 straight to mainnet as if it's the first and only version. Safe to do. The reason to use V2 over V1 is straightforward: V1 required a trusted oracle key to submit match results. If that key was ever compromised, someone could fake tournament outcomes. V2 requires the actual players to sign — ecrecover makes it cryptographically impossible for anyone, including CrossRealm, to fake a result without the players' private keys. For a platform built around trustless settlement that's not just better, it's consistent with everything else you've built. The one thing to be aware of: V2 requires the three guardian addresses in the constructor. Those are already set in the deploy script as your three Safe signer wallets. Guardians can only resolve disputes when a loser refuses to sign — they cannot initiate payouts, move funds, or fake results. Their power is narrow by design. So to directly answer your question: yes, drop the original TournamentBracket.sol from your contracts folder entirely and use TournamentBracketV2.sol only. The deploy script already references V2. i am i not supposed to compile first before running this : npx hardhat run scripts/deploy.js --network core 5:24 AM Weighed automatic compilation against best practices Weighed automatic compilation against best practices Hardhat actually compiles automatically when you run the deploy script — if artifacts are missing or any contract has changed, it recompiles before executing. So technically you don't have to. But you should compile first separately anyway, for one important reason: if there are any compilation errors, they surface cleanly with exact file and line numbers. If you go straight to deploy and there's a compile error, it fails mid-run and you have to untangle what deployed and what didn't. The correct order is: bash npx hardhat compile Fix any errors it reports, then once it says "Compiled X contracts successfully": bash npx hardhat run scripts/deploy.js --network core The most likely compile issues you'll hit given your setup: Duplicate contract names — if your contracts folder still has the old TournamentBracket.sol alongside TournamentBracketV2.sol, or old RaceContract.sol alongside RaceContractV3.sol, Hardhat will complain. Remove the old versions before compiling. Missing imports — the LP vault and rewards contracts import OpenZeppelin upgradeable packages. If those aren't installed run: bash npm install @openzeppelin/contracts-upgradeable Stack too deep — your config already handles this with viaIR: true, so you should be fine. Pragma mismatch — your config compiles everything as 0.8.24. All the new contracts are 0.8.20 or 0.8.24 so they'll compile under 0.8.24 without issue since Solidity is backwards compatible within the same major version. Once npx hardhat compile exits clean with no errors or warnings you care about, then run the deploy. Do not interact with them in any way. The Lorem Ipsum text is a giveaway — scammers airdrop fake NFTs to thousands of wallets simultaneously. The "ETH dividend" label is the trap. It's designed to make you think you have unclaimed earnings, so you click something, visit a website, or approve a transaction — at which point the contract drains your wallet. What to do: absolutely nothing. Don't transfer them, don't try to burn them, don't visit any website linked in the metadata, don't click "claim dividend", don't approve any transaction related to that contract address 0xe944F...a902C. Interacting with scam contracts in any way can trigger malicious approval functions that give the contract permission to spend your real tokens. They are completely harmless as long as you ignore them. Having them sitting in your wallet does nothing. They cannot steal your REALM or CORE just by existing there. Now ignore those entirely and focus on the LP burn. The PrivateLPVault_v2 contract is built for V2 because the router 0xBb5e1777A331ED93E07cF043363e1d7d924e050a is the V2 router. All 18 deployed contract addresses are hardcoded in the Contracts tab. It uses ethers v6 to match your project. What each tab does: Race Duel — loser signs their concession, winner submits both to collect the prize. No oracle involved, one transaction settles the whole thing. Circuit Race — each participant signs the same result hash, all signatures collected and submitted together. Tournament — winner signs win claim, loser signs concession, either player submits both. Guardian dispute flow if loser refuses. Score Attack — each player signs their own score individually, relay or any participant submits them all at once. Contracts — full address reference for all 18 deployed contracts plus the 3 pending Safe actions and Step 3 upgrade details. Deploy this as a standalone HTML file to Netlify or include it in your main app. It auto-connects if MetaMask is already authorised and auto-switches to Core Mainnet (Chain ID 1116) if needed. The singleton address 0xd9db270c1b5e3bd161e8c8503c55ceabee709552 confirms this is Gnosis Safe v1.3.0. This matters for one specific reason: in v1.3.0 the Safe uses the v value of the signature to determine how to verify it. If you sign with eth_sign (which MetaMask uses), MetaMask adds the Ethereum prefix internally and returns v = 27 or v = 28. But Safe v1.3.0 interprets v = 27/28 as a raw ECDSA signature over the hash with no prefix — so it will try to recover the signer against the unprefixed hash and get the wrong address, causing GS026: Invalid owner provided revert. The fix: after signing, add 4 to the v byte. This tells Safe v1.3.0 the signature was created with eth_sign (prefixed), so it adjusts its recovery accordingly. v=27 becomes v=31, v=28 becomes v=32. Ran 2 commands, read a file Ran 2 commands, read a file The singleton 0xd9db270c1b5e3bd161e8c8503c55ceabee709552 confirmed Gnosis Safe v1.3.0. Here is exactly what that means for this page and why it matters: Safe v1.3.0 uses EIP-712 with the chain ID baked into the domain separator. The getTransactionHash call goes directly to the contract so it handles that correctly — no manual EIP-712 construction needed. The critical fix is the adjustSigForSafe function. When MetaMask signs via eth_sign it internally applies the \x19Ethereum Signed Message:\n32 prefix to your hash before signing. Safe v1.3.0's checkNSignatures checks the v byte to know whether to expect a prefixed or unprefixed hash during recovery. v=27/28 means unprefixed, v=31/32 means prefixed (eth_sign). So after MetaMask returns the signature, the page adds 4 to v — that single byte change is what makes Safe v1.3.0 recover the correct signer address and pass verification instead of reverting with GS026.
